<p>In 2009 the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_State" title="U.S. Department of State" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Department of State</a>'s <i>Trafficking in Persons Report</i> reported that <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_modern_Africa" title="Slavery in modern Africa" class="mw-redirect">slavery</a> in Burkina Faso continued to exist and that Burkinabè children were often the victims.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> Slavery in the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> states in general, is an entrenched institution with a long history that dates back to the <a href="/wiki/Arab_slave_trade" title="Arab slave trade">Arab slave trade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_groups">Ethnic groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burkina_Faso&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Ethnic groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Burkina_Faso" title="Ethnic groups in Burkina Faso">Ethnic groups in Burkina Faso</a></div>
<p>Burkina Faso's 17.3&#160;million people belong to two major West African ethnic cultural groups—the <a href="/wiki/Gur_languages" title="Gur languages">Voltaic</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mand%C3%A9_peoples" title="Mandé peoples">Mande</a> (whose common language is <a href="/wiki/Dioula_language" title="Dioula language" class="mw-redirect">Dioula</a>). The Voltaic <a href="/wiki/Mossi_people" title="Mossi people">Mossi</a> make up about one-half of the population. The Mossi claim descent from warriors who migrated to present-day Burkina Faso from the area of <a href="/wiki/Ghana_Empire" title="Ghana Empire">Ghana Empire</a> about 1100. They established an empire that lasted more than 800 years. Predominantly farmers, the Mossi kingdom is led by the <i><a href="/wiki/Mogho_Naba" title="Mogho Naba">Mogho Naba</a>,</i> whose court is in Ouagadougou.<sup id="cite_ref-bn_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burkina_Faso&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Burkina_Faso" title="Languages of Burkina Faso">Languages of Burkina Faso</a></div>
<p>Burkina Faso is a <a href="/wiki/Multilingual" title="Multilingual" class="mw-redirect">multilingual</a> country. An estimated 69 languages are spoken there,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> of which about 60 are <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_language" title="Indigenous language">indigenous</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Mossi_language" title="Mossi language">Mossi language</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mossi_language" title="Mossi language">Mossi</a>: <span lang="mos"><i>Mòoré</i></span>) is spoken by about 40% of the population, mainly in the central region around the capital, Ouagadougou, along with other, closely related <a href="/wiki/Gurunsi_languages" title="Gurunsi languages">Gurunsi languages</a> scattered throughout Burkina.</p>
<p>In the west, <a href="/wiki/Mande_languages" title="Mande languages">Mande languages</a> are widely spoken, the most predominant being <a href="/wiki/Dyula_language" title="Dyula language">Dyula</a> (also known as Jula or Dioula), others including <a href="/wiki/Bobo_language" title="Bobo language">Bobo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samo_language_(Burkina)" title="Samo language (Burkina)">Samo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marka_language" title="Marka language">Marka</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Fula_language" title="Fula language">Fula language</a> (<a href="/wiki/Fula_language" title="Fula language">Fula</a>: <span lang="ff"><i>Fulfulde</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <span lang="fr"><i>Peuhl</i></span>) is widespread, particularly in the north. The <a href="/wiki/Gourmanch%C3%A9_language" title="Gourmanché language">Gourmanché language</a> is spoken in the east, while the <a href="/wiki/Bissa_language" title="Bissa language">Bissa language</a> is spoken in the south.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> is <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>, which was introduced during the colonial period. French is the principal language of administrative, political and judicial institutions, public services, and the press. It is the only language for laws, administration and courts.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burkina_Faso&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Moschee_von_Bobo-Dioulasso.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Moschee_von_Bobo-Dioulasso.jpg/250px-Moschee_von_Bobo-Dioulasso.jpg" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Moschee_von_Bobo-Dioulasso.jpg/375px-Moschee_von_Bobo-Dioulasso.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Moschee_von_Bobo-Dioulasso.jpg/500px-Moschee_von_Bobo-Dioulasso.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3354" data-file-height="2236" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Moschee_von_Bobo-Dioulasso.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Grand Mosque of <a href="/wiki/Bobo-Dioulasso" title="Bobo-Dioulasso">Bobo-Dioulasso</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Burkina_Faso" title="Religion in Burkina Faso">Religion in Burkina Faso</a></div>
<p>Statistics on religion in Burkina Faso are inexact because <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> are often practiced in tandem with indigenous religious beliefs. The <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Burkina_Faso" title="Government of Burkina Faso" class="mw-redirect">Government of Burkina Faso</a> 2006 census reported that 60.5% of the population practice Islam, and that the majority of this group belong to the <a href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni" class="mw-redirect">Sunni</a> branch,<sup id="cite_ref-RGPH2006_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RGPH2006-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-report2010_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-report2010-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> while a small minority adheres to <a href="/wiki/Shia" title="Shia" class="mw-redirect">Shia</a> Islam.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> There are also large concentrations of the <a href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya" title="Ahmadiyya">Ahmadiyya</a> Muslims.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A significant number of Sunni Muslims identify with the <a href="/wiki/Tijaniyah" title="Tijaniyah" class="mw-redirect">Tijaniyah</a> <a href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi" class="mw-redirect">Sufi</a> order. The government estimated that 23.2% of the population are Christians (19% being Roman Catholics and 4.2% members of Protestant denominations); 15.3% follow <a href="/wiki/African_traditional_religion" title="African traditional religion" class="mw-redirect">traditional indigenous beliefs</a>, 0.6% have other religions, and 0.4% have none (atheism is virtually nonexistent).<sup id="cite_ref-RGPH2006_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RGPH2006-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-report2010_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-report2010-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A popular saying in Burkina Faso claims that "50% are Muslim, 50% are Christian, and 100% are <a href="/wiki/Animist" title="Animist" class="mw-redirect">animist</a>". This shows the wide level of acceptance of the religions amongst each other. Muslims and Christians also value ancient animist rites. The Great Mosque of <a href="/wiki/Bobo-Dioulasso" title="Bobo-Dioulasso">Bobo-Dioulasso</a> was built by people of different faiths working together.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burkina_Faso&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Burkina_Faso" title="Health in Burkina Faso">Health in Burkina Faso</a></div>
<p>In 2012, the average life expectancy was estimated at 57 for male and 59 for female. The under five mortality rate and the infant mortality rate were respectively 102 and 66 per 1000 live births.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2014, the median age of its inhabitants is 17 and the estimated population growth rate is 3.05%.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 2011, health expenditures was 6.5% of GDP; the maternal mortality ratio was estimated at 300 deaths per 100000 live births and the physician density at 0.05/1000 population in 2010. In 2012, it was estimated that the adult HIV <a href="/wiki/Prevalence" title="Prevalence">prevalence</a> rate (ages 15–49) was 1.0%.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> According to the 2011 UNAIDS Report, HIV prevalence is declining among pregnant women who attend antenatal clinics.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> According to a 2005 World Health Organization report, an estimated 72.5% of Burkina Faso's girls and women have suffered <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">female genital mutilation</a>, administered according to traditional rituals.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Central government spending on health was 3% in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> As of 2009, studies estimated there were as few as 10 physicians per 100,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> In addition, there were 41 nurses and 13 midwives per 100,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Demographic_and_Health_Surveys" title="Demographic and Health Surveys">Demographic and Health Surveys</a> has completed three surveys in Burkina Faso since 1993, and had another in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-Burkina_Surveys_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burkina_Surveys-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burkina_Faso&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Burkina_Faso" title="Education in Burkina Faso">Education in Burkina Faso</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gando-School-Burkina-Faso.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Gando-School-Burkina-Faso.JPG/220px-Gando-School-Burkina-Faso.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Gando-School-Burkina-Faso.JPG/330px-Gando-School-Burkina-Faso.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Gando-School-Burkina-Faso.JPG/440px-Gando-School-Burkina-Faso.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2692" data-file-height="1795" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gando-School-Burkina-Faso.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Gando,_Burkina_Faso" title="Gando, Burkina Faso">Gando</a> primary school. Its architect, <a href="/wiki/Di%C3%A9b%C3%A9do_Francis_K%C3%A9r%C3%A9" title="Diébédo Francis Kéré">Diébédo Francis Kéré</a>, received the <a href="/wiki/Aga_Khan_Award_for_Architecture" title="Aga Khan Award for Architecture">Aga Khan Award for Architecture</a> in 2004.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Education in Burkina Faso is divided into primary, secondary and higher education.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> High school costs approximately CFA 25,000 ($50 USD) per year, which is far above the means of most Burkinabè families. Boys receive preference in schooling; as such, girls' education and literacy rates are far lower than their male counterparts. An increase in girls' schooling has been observed because of the government's policy of making school cheaper for girls and granting them more scholarships.</p>
<p>To proceed from elementary to middle school, middle to high school or high school to college, national exams must be passed. Institutions of higher education include the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Ouagadougou" title="University of Ouagadougou">University of Ouagadougou</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Polytechnic_University_of_Bobo-Dioulasso" title="The Polytechnic University of Bobo-Dioulasso" class="mw-redirect">The Polytechnic University of Bobo-Dioulasso</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Koudougou" title="University of Koudougou">University of Koudougou</a>, which is also a teacher training institution. There are some small private colleges in the capital city of <a href="/wiki/Ouagadougou" title="Ouagadougou">Ouagadougou</a> but these are affordable to only a small portion of the population.</p>
<p>There is also the <a href="/wiki/International_School_of_Ouagadougou" title="International School of Ouagadougou">International School of Ouagadougou</a> (ISO), an American-based private school located in Ouagadougou.</p>
<p>The 2008 UN Development Program Report ranked Burkina Faso as the country with the lowest level of literacy in the world, despite a concerted effort to double its literacy rate from 12.8% in 1990 to 25.3% in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burkina_Faso&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Burkina_Faso" title="Culture of Burkina Faso">Culture of Burkina Faso</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_Nunuma_of_Winiama_maskerdanser_TMnr_20031569.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_Nunuma_of_Winiama_maskerdanser_TMnr_20031569.jpg/250px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_Nunuma_of_Winiama_maskerdanser_TMnr_20031569.jpg" width="250" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_Nunuma_of_Winiama_maskerdanser_TMnr_20031569.jpg/375px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_Nunuma_of_Winiama_maskerdanser_TMnr_20031569.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_Nunuma_of_Winiama_maskerdanser_TMnr_20031569.jpg/500px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_Nunuma_of_Winiama_maskerdanser_TMnr_20031569.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="700" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_Nunuma_of_Winiama_maskerdanser_TMnr_20031569.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A masked Winiama dancer, ca.1970</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Literature_in_Burkina_Faso" title="Literature in Burkina Faso" class="mw-redirect">Literature in Burkina Faso</a> is based on the <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral tradition</a>, which remains important. In 1934, during French occupation, Dim-Dolobsom Ouedraogo published his <i>Maximes, pensées et devinettes mossi</i> (<i>Maximes, Thoughts and Riddles of the Mossi</i>), a record of the oral history of the <a href="/wiki/Mossi_people" title="Mossi people">Mossi people</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-KS_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KS-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The oral tradition continued to have an influence on Burkinabè writers in the post-independence Burkina Faso of the 1960s, such as <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Boni" title="Nazi Boni">Nazi Boni</a> and Roger Nikiema.<sup id="cite_ref-TJA_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TJA-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> The 1960s saw a growth in the number of playwrights being published.<sup id="cite_ref-KS_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KS-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> Since the 1970s, literature has developed in Burkina Faso with many more writers being published.<sup id="cite_ref-JM_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JM-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Theatre_of_Burkina_Faso" title="Theatre of Burkina Faso">theatre of Burkina Faso</a> combines traditional Burkinabè performance with the colonial influences and post-colonial efforts to educate rural people to produce a distinctive national theatre. Traditional ritual ceremonies of the many ethnic groups in Burkina Faso have long involved dancing with <a href="/wiki/Mask" title="Mask">masks</a>. Western-style theatre became common during colonial times, heavily influenced by <a href="/wiki/French_theatre" title="French theatre" class="mw-redirect">French theatre</a>. With independence came a new style of theatre inspired by <a href="/wiki/Theatre_of_the_Oppressed" title="Theatre of the Oppressed">forum theatre</a> aimed at educating and entertaining Burkina Faso's rural people.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arts_and_crafts">Arts and crafts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burkina_Faso&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Arts and crafts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Art_of_Burkina_Faso" title="Art of Burkina Faso">Art of Burkina Faso</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burkina_faso_artisan_painted_gourds.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Burkina_faso_artisan_painted_gourds.jpg/220px-Burkina_faso_artisan_painted_gourds.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Burkina_faso_artisan_painted_gourds.jpg/330px-Burkina_faso_artisan_painted_gourds.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Burkina_faso_artisan_painted_gourds.jpg/440px-Burkina_faso_artisan_painted_gourds.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1630" data-file-height="1086" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burkina_faso_artisan_painted_gourds.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artisan garland of decorative painted <a href="/wiki/Gourd" title="Gourd">gourds</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ouagadougou" title="Ouagadougou">Ouagadougou</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In addition to several rich traditional artistic heritages among the peoples, there is a large artist community in Burkina Faso, especially in <a href="/wiki/Ouagadougou" title="Ouagadougou">Ouagadougou</a>. Much of the crafts produced are for the growing tourist industry.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burkina_Faso&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Cuisine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Burkinab%C3%A9_cuisine" title="Burkinabé cuisine">Burkinabé cuisine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fufu.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Fufu.jpg/240px-Fufu.jpg" width="240" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Fufu.jpg/360px-Fufu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Fufu.jpg/480px-Fufu.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fufu.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A plate of <a href="/wiki/Fufu" title="Fufu">fufu</a> (right) accompanied with <a href="/wiki/Peanut_soup" title="Peanut soup">peanut soup</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Typical of West African cuisine, Burkina Faso's cuisine is based on <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple foods</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">sorghum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Millet" title="Millet">millet</a>, rice, maize, <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">peanuts</a>, potatoes, <a href="/wiki/Bean" title="Bean">beans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yam_(vegetable)" title="Yam (vegetable)">yams</a> and <a href="/wiki/Okra" title="Okra">okra</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ox_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ox-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> The most common sources of animal protein are chicken, chicken eggs and fresh water fish. A typical Burkinabè beverage is Banji or Palm Wine, which is fermented <a href="/wiki/Palm_wine" title="Palm wine">palm sap</a> and Zoom-kom[?].</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cinema">Cinema</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burkina_Faso&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Cinema">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Burkina_Faso" title="Cinema of Burkina Faso">Cinema of Burkina Faso</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Burkina_Faso" title="Cinema of Burkina Faso">cinema of Burkina Faso</a> is an important part of West African and African film industry.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> Burkina's contribution to <a href="/wiki/African_cinema" title="African cinema" class="mw-redirect">African cinema</a> started with the establishment of the film festival <a href="/wiki/FESPACO" title="FESPACO" class="mw-redirect">FESPACO</a> (Festival Panafricain du Cinéma et de la Télévision de Ouagadougou), which was launched as a film week in 1969. Many of the nation's filmmakers are known internationally and have won international prizes.</p>
<p>For many years the headquarters of the Federation of Panafrican Filmmakers (FEPACI) was in Ouagadougou, rescued in 1983 from a period of moribund inactivity by the enthusiastic support and funding of President Sankara. (In 2006 the Secretariat of FEPACI moved to South Africa, but the headquarters of the organization is still in Ouagaoudougou.) Among the best known directors from Burkina Faso are <a href="/wiki/Gaston_Kabor%C3%A9" title="Gaston Kaboré">Gaston Kaboré</a>, <a href="/wiki/Idrissa_Ouedraogo" title="Idrissa Ouedraogo">Idrissa Ouedraogo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dani_Kouyate" title="Dani Kouyate" class="mw-redirect">Dani Kouyate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> Burkina produces popular television series such as <i>Bobodjiouf</i>. The internationally known filmmakers such as Ouedraogo, Kabore, Yameogo, and Kouyate make popular television series.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burkina_Faso&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burkina_team.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Burkina_team.png/220px-Burkina_team.png" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Burkina_team.png/330px-Burkina_team.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Burkina_team.png/440px-Burkina_team.png 2x" data-file-width="3136" data-file-height="2232" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burkina_team.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Burkina_Faso_national_football_team" title="Burkina Faso national football team">Burkina Faso national football team</a> in white playing a football match.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Burkina_Faso" title="Sport in Burkina Faso">Sport in Burkina Faso</a></div>
<p>Sport in Burkina Faso is widespread and includes football (soccer), basketball, cycling, Rugby union, handball, tennis, athletics, boxing and martial arts. Football is very popular in Burkina Faso, played both professionally, and informally in towns and villages across the country. The national team is nicknamed "Les Etalons" ("the Stallions") in reference to the legendary horse of Princess <a href="/wiki/Yennenga" title="Yennenga">Yennenga</a>.</p>
<p>In 1998, Burkina Faso hosted the <a href="/wiki/Africa_Cup_of_Nations" title="Africa Cup of Nations">Africa Cup of Nations</a> for which the Omnisport Stadium in Bobo-Dioulasso was built. In 2013, Burkina Faso qualified for the African Cup of Nations in South Africa, reached the final, but then lost to Nigeria by the score of 0 to 1. The country is currently ranked 71st in the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Rankings" title="FIFA World Rankings">FIFA World Rankings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burkina_Faso&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burkina_Faso_media_2010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Burkina_Faso_media_2010.jpg/220px-Burkina_Faso_media_2010.jpg" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Burkina_Faso_media_2010.jpg/330px-Burkina_Faso_media_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Burkina_Faso_media_2010.jpg/440px-Burkina_Faso_media_2010.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2700" data-file-height="2160" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burkina_Faso_media_2010.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A member of the Burkinabé media at work in <a href="/wiki/Ouagadougou" title="Ouagadougou">Ouagadougou</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Media_of_Burkina_Faso" title="Media of Burkina Faso">Media of Burkina Faso</a> and <a href="/wiki/Communications_in_Burkina_Faso" title="Communications in Burkina Faso" class="mw-redirect">Communications in Burkina Faso</a></div>
<p>The nation's principal media outlet is its state-sponsored combined television and radio service, <i>Radiodiffusion-Télévision Burkina</i> (RTB).<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>RTB</i> broadcasts on two <a href="/wiki/Medium-wave" title="Medium-wave" class="mw-redirect">medium-wave</a> (<a href="/wiki/Amplitude_modulation" title="Amplitude modulation">AM</a>) and several <a href="/wiki/Frequency_modulation" title="Frequency modulation">FM</a> frequencies. Besides RTB, there are privately owned sports, cultural, music, and religious FM radio stations. <i>RTB</i> maintains a worldwide short-wave news broadcast (<i>Radio Nationale Burkina</i>) in the French language from the capital at Ouagadougou using a 100&#160;kW transmitter on 4.815 and 5.030&#160;MHz.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Attempts to develop an independent press and media in Burkina Faso have been intermittent. In 1998, investigative journalist <a href="/wiki/Norbert_Zongo" title="Norbert Zongo">Norbert Zongo</a>, his brother Ernest, his driver, and another man were assassinated by unknown assailants, and the bodies burned. The crime was never solved.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> However, an independent Commission of Inquiry later concluded that Norbert Zongo was killed for political reasons because of his investigative work into the death of David Ouedraogo, a chauffeur who worked for François Compaoré, President Blaise Compaoré's brother.<sup id="cite_ref-en.rsf.org_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-en.rsf.org-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Outrageous_Denial_Of_Justice_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Outrageous_Denial_Of_Justice-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In January 1999, François Compaoré was charged with the murder of David Ouedraogo, who had died as a result of torture in January 1998. The charges were later dropped by a military tribunal after an appeal. In August 2000, five members of the President's personal security guard detail (<i>Régiment de la Sécurité Présidentielle</i>, or RSP) were charged with the murder of Ouedraogo. RSP members Marcel Kafando, Edmond Koama, and Ousseini Yaro, investigated as suspects in the Norbert Zongo assassination, were convicted in the Ouedraogo case and sentenced to lengthy prison terms.<sup id="cite_ref-en.rsf.org_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-en.rsf.org-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Outrageous_Denial_Of_Justice_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Outrageous_Denial_Of_Justice-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Since the death of Norbert Zongo, several protests regarding the Zongo investigation and treatment of journalists have been prevented or dispersed by government police and security forces. In April 2007, popular radio reggae host <a href="/wiki/Sams%E2%80%99K_Le_Jah" title="Sams’K Le Jah">Karim Sama</a>, whose programs feature reggae songs interspersed with critical commentary on alleged government injustice and corruption, received several death threats.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Sama's personal car was later burned outside the private radio station <i>Ouaga FM</i> by unknown vandals.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> In response, the <a href="/wiki/Committee_to_Protect_Journalists" title="Committee to Protect Journalists">Committee to Protect Journalists</a> (CPJ) wrote to President Compaoré to request his government investigate the sending of e-mailed death threats to journalists and radio commentators in Burkina Faso who were critical of the government.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> In December 2008, police in Ouagadougou questioned leaders of a protest march that called for a renewed investigation into the unsolved Zongo assassination. Among the marchers was Jean-Claude Meda, the president of the Association of Journalists of Burkina Faso.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

Cambridge: New York.</span><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABurkina+Faso&amp;rft.aufirst=Patrick&amp;rft.aulast=Manning&amp;rft.au=Manning%2C+Patrick&amp;rft.btitle=Francophone+Sub-Saharan+Africa%3A+1880-198&amp;rft.date=1988&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.place=Cambridge&amp;rft.pub=New+York&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The name is an <a href="/wiki/Amalgamation_(linguistics)" title="Amalgamation (linguistics)" class="mw-redirect">amalgam</a> of <a href="/wiki/More_language" title="More language" class="mw-redirect">More</a> <i>burkina</i> ("honest", "upright", or "incorruptible men") and <a href="/wiki/Dioula_language" title="Dioula language" class="mw-redirect">Jula</a> <i>faso</i> ("homeland"; literally "father's house"). The "-be" suffix in the name for the people – Burkinabe – comes from the <a href="/wiki/Fula_language" title="Fula language">Fula</a> plural suffix for people, <i>-ɓe</i>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><span class="citation book">Kasuka, Bridgette (2011). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://books.google.com/books?id=80SvCmSTxGoC&amp;pg=PA128&amp;lpg=PA128&amp;dq=Deterioration+in+relations+with+neighbouring+countries+was+one+of+the+reasons+given+by+Compaor%C3%A9+for+the+coup&amp;source=bl&amp;ots=hP6fmRRghm&amp;sig=IsJH0AnwaSoZGfFDS9kMXD7yRTI&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=BZVSVNi6BviIsQTc-YLIAQ&amp;ved=0CC4Q6AEwAg#v=onepage&amp;q=Deterioration%20in%20relations%20with%20neighbouring%20countries%20was%20one%20of%20the%20reasons%20given%20by%20Compaor%C3%A9%20for%20the%20coup&amp;f=false"><i>African Leaders</i></a>. 3rd Paragraph: Bankole Kamara Taylor. p.&#160;13. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1468114362" title="Special:BookSources/978-1468114362">978-1468114362</a><span class="reference-accessdate">.
